</p><p>A test ban seemed particularly close in 1960, with Britain and France in accord with the US (though France conducted its <a href="/wiki/Gerboise_Bleue" title="Gerboise Bleue">first nuclear test</a> in February) and the Soviet Union having largely accepted the Macmillan-Eisenhower proposal. But US-Soviet relations soured after an American <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">U-2 spy plane</a> was <a href="/wiki/1960_U-2_incident" title="1960 U-2 incident">shot down</a> in Soviet airspace in May 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The Paris summit was abruptly cancelled and the Soviet Union withdrew from the seismic research group, which subsequently dissolved. Meetings of the Geneva Conference continued until December, but little progress was made as Western-Soviet relations continued to grow more antagonistic through the summer, punctuated by the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">Congo Crisis</a> in July and angry exchanges at the UN in September.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeaborg198124_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeaborg198124-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Macmillan would later claim to President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> that the failure to achieve a test ban in 1960 "was all the fault of the American 'big hole' obsession and the consequent insistence on a wantonly large number of on-site inspections."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966345_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966345-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002452_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002452-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower would leave office with an agreement out of reach, as Eisenhower's technical advisors, upon whom he relied heavily, became mired in the complex technical questions of a test ban, driven in part by a strong interest among American experts to lower the error rate of seismic test detection technology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013249,_305_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013249,_305-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreene20066–8_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreene20066–8-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Some, including Kistiakowsky, would eventually raise concerns about the ability of inspections and monitors to successfully detect tests.<sup id="cite_ref-fas_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fas-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The primary product of negotiations under Eisenhower was the testing moratorium without any enforcement mechanism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis1982193_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis1982193-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, the goal of a comprehensive test ban would be abandoned in favor of a partial ban due to questions over seismic detection of underground tests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013249,_305_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013249,_305-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Political scientist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gilpin" title="Robert Gilpin">Robert Gilpin</a> later argued that Eisenhower faced three camps in the push for a test ban.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilpin1962_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilpin1962-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The first was the "control" camp, led by figures like Linus Pauling and astronomer <a href="/wiki/Harlow_Shapley" title="Harlow Shapley">Harlow Shapley</a>, which believed that both testing and possession of nuclear weapons was dangerous. Second, there was the "finite containment" camp, populated by scientists like Hans Bethe, which was concerned by perceived Soviet aggression but still believed that a test ban would be workable with adequate verification measures. Third, the "infinite containment" camp, of which Strauss, Teller, and members of the defense establishment were members, believed that any test ban would grant the Soviet Union the ability to conduct secret tests and move ahead in the arms race.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolsby198461–63_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolsby198461–63-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The degree of Eisenhower's interest in a test ban is a matter of some historical dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreene20061_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreene20061-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_E._Ambrose" title="Stephen E. Ambrose">Stephen E. Ambrose</a> writes that by early 1960, a test ban had become "the major goal of his President, indeed of his entire career," and would be "his final and most lasting gift to his country."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmbrose1991504_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmbrose1991504-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Conversely, <a href="/wiki/John_Lewis_Gaddis" title="John Lewis Gaddis">John Lewis Gaddis</a> characterizes negotiations of the 1950s as "an embarrassing series of American reversals," suggesting a lack of real US commitment to arms control efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis1982192–193_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis1982192–193-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The historian Robert Divine also attributed the failure to achieve a deal to Eisenhower's "lack of leadership," evidenced by his inability to overcome paralyzing differences among US diplomats, military leaders, national security experts, and scientists on the subject. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Nitze" title="Paul Nitze">Paul Nitze</a> would similarly suggest that Eisenhower never formulated a cohesive test ban policy, noting his ability to "believe in two mutually contradictory and inconsistent propositions at the same time."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013250_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013250-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewed_efforts">Renewed efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Renewed efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_and_Khrushchev_at_Vienna_Meeting_-_NARA_-_193203.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Kennedy_and_Khrushchev_at_Vienna_Meeting_-_NARA_-_193203.jpg/250px-Kennedy_and_Khrushchev_at_Vienna_Meeting_-_NARA_-_193203.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Kennedy_and_Khrushchev_at_Vienna_Meeting_-_NARA_-_193203.jpg/375px-Kennedy_and_Khrushchev_at_Vienna_Meeting_-_NARA_-_193203.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Kennedy_and_Khrushchev_at_Vienna_Meeting_-_NARA_-_193203.jpg/500px-Kennedy_and_Khrushchev_at_Vienna_Meeting_-_NARA_-_193203.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2014" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_and_Khrushchev_at_Vienna_Meeting_-_NARA_-_193203.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">Kennedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Khrushchev</a> in Vienna</div></div></div>
<p>Upon assuming the presidency in January 1961, John F. Kennedy was committed to pursuing a comprehensive test ban and ordered a review of the American negotiating position in an effort to accelerate languishing talks, believing Eisenhower's approach to have been "insufficient."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013305_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013305-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002304_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002304-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeaborg198130_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeaborg198130-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In making his case for a test ban, Kennedy drew a direct link between continued testing and nuclear proliferation, calling it the "'Nth-country' problem." While a candidate, Kennedy had argued, "For once <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, or France, or <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, or half a dozen other nations successfully test an atomic bomb, the security of both Russians and Americans is dangerously weakened." He had also claimed that renewed testing would be "damaging to the American image" and might threaten the "existence of human life." On the campaign trail, Kennedy's test-ban proposal consisted of a continued US testing moratorium, expanded efforts to reach a comprehensive agreement, limit any future tests to those minimizing fallout, and expand research on fallout.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013306–307_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013306–307-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeaborg198132_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeaborg198132-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Notably, early in his term, Kennedy also presided over a significant increase in defense spending, which was reciprocated by the Soviet Union shortly thereafter, thus placing the test-ban negotiations in the context of an accelerating arms race.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966339–341_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966339–341-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 21 March 1961, test-ban negotiations resumed in Geneva and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Dean_(lawyer)" title="Arthur Dean (lawyer)">Arthur Dean</a>, a lead US envoy,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> offered a new proposal in an attempt to bridge the gap between the two sides. The early Kennedy proposal largely grew out of later Eisenhower efforts, with a ban on all tests but low-yield underground ones (below magnitude 4.75), which would be subject to a three-year moratorium.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolsby198469_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolsby198469-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The US and UK proposed 20 on-site inspections per annum, while the Soviet Union proposed three. The verification procedures included in the Anglo-American plan were unacceptable to Tsarapkin, who responded with separate proposals rejected by the Western powers.<sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Specifically, the Soviet Union proposed a "troika" mechanism: a monitoring board composed of representatives of the West, the Soviet Union, and <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">nonaligned states</a> that would require unanimity before acting (effectively giving the Soviet Union veto authority).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolsby198469_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolsby198469-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002305–306,_369_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002305–306,_369-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In May 1961, Kennedy attempted via secret contact between Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a> and a Soviet intelligence officer to settle on 15 inspections per year. This was rejected by Khrushchev.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199978_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199978-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ahead of the June 1961 <a href="/wiki/Vienna_summit" title="Vienna summit">Vienna summit</a> between Kennedy and Khrushchev, Robert F. Kennedy spoke with the Soviet ambassador to the US, who suggested that progress on a test ban was possible in a direct meeting between the leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002348_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002348-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> President Kennedy subsequently announced to the press that he had "strong hopes" for progress on a test ban.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002356_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002356-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In Vienna, Khrushchev suggested that three inspections per year would have to be the limit, as anything more frequent would constitute <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">espionage</a>. Khrushchev privately believed allowing three inspections to be a significant concession to the West, as other Soviet officials preferred an even less intrusive system, and was angered by US resistance. Khrushchev later told his son, "hold out a finger to them—they chop off your whole hand."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199979_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199979-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, the Soviet Union had once been ready to support an control commission under the aegis of the UN, Khrushchev explained, but it could not longer do so given perceived bias in recent UN action in the Congo.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002369_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002369-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Instead, Khrushchev reiterated the troika proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002305–306,_369_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002305–306,_369-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, Khrushchev insisted that the test ban be considered in the context of "general and complete disarmament," arguing that a test ban on its own was unimportant; Kennedy said the US could only agree with a guarantee that a disarmament agreement would be reached quickly (the Vienna demands thus amounted to a reversal of both sides' earlier positions).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolsby198469_88-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolsby198469-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy also disagreed that a test ban was itself insignificant; the world could expect many more countries in the coming years to cross the nuclear threshold without a test ban. Ultimately, the two leaders left Vienna without clear progress on the subject.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002369–370_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002369–370-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The Soviet Union would drop the general-disarmament demand in November 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-statedept_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Lifting_the_moratorium:_1961.E2.80.9362"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifting_the_moratorium:_1961–62">Lifting the moratorium: 1961–62</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Lifting the moratorium: 1961–62">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Following the setback in Vienna and <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Crisis_of_1961" title="Berlin Crisis of 1961">Berlin Crisis of 1961</a>, as well as the Soviet decision to resume testing in August (attributed by Moscow to a changed international situation and French nuclear tests), Kennedy faced mounting pressure from the Department of Defense and nuclear laboratories to set aside the dream of a test ban. In June 1961, following stalled talks in Geneva, Kennedy had argued that Soviet negotiating behavior raised "a serious question about how long we can safely continue on a voluntary basis a refusal to undertake tests in this country without any assurance that the Russians are not testing." Whether or not the Soviet Union had actually conducted secret tests was a matter of debate within the Kennedy administration. A team led by physicist <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_K._H._Panofsky" title="Wolfgang K. H. Panofsky">Wolfgang K. H. Panofsky</a> reported that while the Soviet Union could have secretly tested weapons, there was no evidence indicating that it actually had. Panofsky's findings were dismissed by the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff">Joint Chiefs of Staff</a> as "assertive, ambiguous, semiliterate and generally unimpressive."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013307_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013307-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002455–456_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002455–456-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two weeks after the lifting of the Soviet moratorium in August 1961, and after another failed Anglo-American attempt to have the Soviet Union agree to an atmospheric-test ban, the US restarted testing on 15 September 1961. Kennedy specifically limited such testing to underground and laboratory tests, but under mounting pressure as Soviet tests continued — during the time period of the Soviet <i><a href="/wiki/Tsar_Bomba" title="Tsar Bomba">Tsar Bomba</a></i> 50 Mt+ test detonation on 30 October over <a href="/wiki/Novaya_Zemlya" title="Novaya Zemlya">Novaya Zemlya</a> — Kennedy announced and dedicated funds to a renewed atmospheric testing program in November 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013307_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013307-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966343–344_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966343–344-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A report on the 1961 Soviet tests, published by a group of American scientists led by Hans Bethe, determined "that [Soviet] laboratories had probably been working full speed during the whole moratorium on the assumption that tests would at some time be resume," with preparations likely having begun prior to the resumption of talks in Geneva in March 1961. In January 1962, Bethe, who had once supported a test ban, publicly argued that a ban was "no longer a desirable goal" and the US should test weapons developed by its laboratories. In contrast to Soviet laboratories, US laboratories had been relatively inactive on nuclear weapons issues during the moratorium.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966342–343_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966342–343-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hatless_Jack.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hatless_Jack.jpg/220px-Hatless_Jack.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hatless_Jack.jpg/330px-Hatless_Jack.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hatless_Jack.jpg/440px-Hatless_Jack.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="534" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hatless_Jack.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Macmillan</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">Kennedy</a> in Bermuda</div></div></div>
<p>In December 1961, Macmillan met with Kennedy in <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>, appealing for a final and permanent halt to tests. Kennedy, conversely, used the meeting to request permission to test on <a href="/wiki/Kiritimati" title="Kiritimati">Christmas Island</a>, with US testing grounds in the Pacific having largely been exhausted. Macmillan agreed to seek to give US permission "if the situation did not change." Christmas Island was ultimately opened to US use by February 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966344–345,_347_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966344–345,_347-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On this matter of resumed atmospheric tests, Kennedy lacked the full backing of his administration and allies. In particularly, Macmillan, Adlai Stevenson (then the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_United_Nations" title="United States Ambassador to the United Nations">UN ambassador</a>), the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Information_Agency" title="United States Information Agency">United States Information Agency</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Wiesner" title="Jerome Wiesner">Jerome Wiesner</a>, the PSAC chairman, opposed resuming atmospheric tests. On the side advocating resumption were the AEC, Joint Committee on Atomic Energy, Joint Chiefs of Staff (which had called for renewed atmospheric tests in October 1961), and Department of Defense, though then-Secretary of Defense <a href="/wiki/Robert_McNamara" title="Robert McNamara">Robert McNamara</a> privately acknowledged that such tests were "not really necessary." Teller continued to advocate for atmospheric tests, as well, arguing in early 1962 that nuclear fallout was nothing be concerned about. Teller also argued that testing was necessary to continued advancement of US nuclear capabilities, particularly in terms of the mobility of its weapons and, accordingly, its <a href="/wiki/Second_strike" title="Second strike">second-strike capability</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966345–346_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966345–346-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite Teller's reassurances, Kennedy himself "hated the idea of reopening the race" and was uneasy with continued production of fallout,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002455_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002455-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> a negative consequence of resumed testing that its opponents within the administration stressed. Opponents of the tests also argued that renewed atmospheric tests would come at a significant moral cost to the US, given broad public opposition to the plan, and claimed that further tests were largely unnecessary, with the US already having an adequate nuclear arsenal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966346–347_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966346–347-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Arthur Dean believed that public opposition to atmospheric testing was so great that the US would have to halt such tests within four years even without an agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199033_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199033-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Kenneth_Galbraith" title="John Kenneth Galbraith">John Kenneth Galbraith</a>, then the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_India" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Ambassador to India">ambassador to India</a>, had advised Kennedy in June 1961 that resumed testing "would cause us the gravest difficulties in Asia, Africa and elsewhere." Similarly, Hubert Humphrey described the moratorium as "a ray of hope to millions of worried people." Its termination, Humphrey warned, "might very well turn the political tides in the world in behalf of the Soviets."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002455_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002455-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ultimately, Kennedy sided with those arguing for resumed testing. In particular, an argument by William C. Foster, the head of the <a href="/wiki/Arms_Control_and_Disarmament_Agency" title="Arms Control and Disarmament Agency">Arms Control and Disarmament Agency</a>, swayed Kennedy. Foster argued that if the US failed to respond to the Soviet test series, Moscow could order a second test series, which could give the Soviet Union a significant advantage. Furthermore, a second test series, without US reciprocation, could damage the push for a test ban and make <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> ratification of any agreement less likely.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966347–348_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966347–348-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> On 2 March 1962, building on the November 1961 announcement, Kennedy promised to resume atmospheric testing by the end of April 1962 if Moscow continued to resist the Anglo-American test-ban proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> To an extent, the announcement was a compromise, as Kennedy restricted atmospheric tests to those tests which were "absolutely necessary," not feasible underground, and minimized fallout. The condition that testing would resume only if the Soviet Union continued to oppose the Anglo-American proposal also served as a concession to dissenting voices within his administration and to Macmillan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966347–348_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966347–348-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy portrayed resumed testing as a necessary for the image of US resolve. If the US failed to respond to the Soviet test series, Kennedy explained, Moscow would "chalk it up, not to goodwill, but to a failure of will—not to our confidence in Western superiority, but to our fear of world opinion." Keeping the US in a position of strength, Kennedy argued, would be necessary for a test ban to ever come about.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966349_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966349-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The US suspension of atmospheric tests was lifted on 25 April 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By March 1962, the trilateral talks in Geneva had shifted to 18-party talks at the UN Disarmament Conference.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199019–20_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199019–20-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> On 27 August 1962, within that conference, the US and UK offered two draft treaties to the Soviet Union. The primary proposal included a comprehensive ban verified by control posts under national command, but international supervision, and required on-site inspections. This was rejected by the Soviet Union due to the inspection requirement. The alternative proposal included a partial test ban—underground tests would be excluded—to be verified by national detection mechanisms, without supervision by a supranational body.<sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolsby198471_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolsby198471-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Cuban_Missile_Crisis_and_beyond:_1962.E2.80.9363"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuban_Missile_Crisis_and_beyond:_1962–63">Cuban Missile Crisis and beyond: 1962–63</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cuban Missile Crisis and beyond: 1962–63">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In October 1962, the US and Soviet Union experienced the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a>, which brought the two superpowers to the edge of nuclear war and prompted both Kennedy and Khrushchev to seek accelerated <a href="/wiki/Rapprochement" title="Rapprochement">rapprochement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-atomicheritage_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atomicheritage-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013307_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013307-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cuba_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cuba-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cuba2_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cuba2-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199023–24_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199023–24-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199982_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199982-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> After years of dormant or lethargic negotiations, American and British negotiators subsequently forged a strong working relationship and with Soviet negotiators found common ground on test restrictions later in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> After years of pursuing a comprehensive ban, Khrushchev was convinced to accept a partial ban, partly due to the efforts of Soviet nuclear scientists, including Kurchatov, Sakharov, and <a href="/wiki/Yulii_Borisovich_Khariton" class="mw-redirect" title="Yulii Borisovich Khariton">Yulii Borisovich Khariton</a>, who argued that atmospheric testing had severe consequences for human health.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013307_112-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013307-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199983_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199983-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Khrushchev had been concerned by a partial ban due to the greater US experience in underground tests; by 1962, the US had conducted 89 such tests and the Soviet Union just two (the Soviet focus had been on cheaper, larger-yield atmospheric tests). For this reason, many in the Soviet weapons industry argued that a partial ban would give the US the advantage in nuclear capabilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199981–82_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199981–82-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Khrushchev would later recount that he saw test-ban negotiations as a prime venue for ameliorating tensions after the crisis in Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199024_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199024-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shocked by how close the world had come to thermonuclear war, Khrushchev proposed easing of tensions with the US.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> In a letter to President Kennedy dated 30 October 1962, Kurshchev outlined a range of bold initiatives to forestall the possibility of nuclear war, including proposing a non-aggression treaty between the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a> (NATO) and the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> or even the disbanding these military blocs, a treaty to cease all nuclear weapons testing and even the elimination of all nuclear weapons, resolution of the hot-button issue of Germany by both East and West formally accepting the existence of <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>, and US recognition of the government of mainland China. The letter invited counter-proposals and further exploration of these and other issues through peaceful negotiations. Khrushschev invited <a href="/wiki/Norman_Cousins" title="Norman Cousins">Norman Cousins</a>, the editor of a major US periodical and an anti-nuclear weapons activist, to serve as liaison with President Kennedy, and Cousins met with Khrushchev for four hours in December 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>  Kennedy's response to Khrushchev's proposals was lukewarm but Kennedy expressed to Cousins that he felt constrained in exploring these issues due to pressure from hardliners in the US national security apparatus. However Kennedy pursued negotiations for a partial nuclear test ban.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 13 November 1962, Tsarapkin indicated that the Soviet Union would accept a proposal drafted by US and Soviet experts involving automated test detection stations ("black boxes") and a limited number of on-site inspections. The two sides disagreed over the number of black boxes, however, as the US sought 12–20 such stations and the Soviet Union rejected any more than three.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolsby198471_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolsby198471-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> On 28 December 1962, Kennedy lowered the US demand to 8–10 stations. On 19 February 1963, the number was lowered further to seven, as Khrushchev continued to insist on no more than three.<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was willing to reduce the number to six, though this was not clearly communicated to the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199980_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199980-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> On 20 April 1963, Khrushchev withdrew support for three inspections entirely.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199021_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199021-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Progress was further complicated in early 1963, as a group in the US Congress called for the Soviet proposal to be discarded in favor of the Geneva System.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolsby198471_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolsby198471-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> On 27 May 1963, 34 US Senators, led by Humphrey and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Dodd" title="Thomas J. Dodd">Thomas J. Dodd</a>, introduced a <a href="/wiki/Simple_resolution" title="Simple resolution">resolution</a> calling for Kennedy to propose another partial ban to the Soviet Union involving national monitoring and no on-site inspections. Absent Soviet agreement, the resolution called for Kennedy to continue to "pursue it with vigor, seeking the widest possible international support" while suspending all atmospheric and underwater tests. The effect of the resolution was to bolster the general push for a test ban, though Kennedy initially was concerned that it would damage attempts to secure a comprehensive ban, and had administration figures (including the Joint Chiefs of Staff) reiterate a call for a comprehensive ban.<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002899–900_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002899–900-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolsby198472_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolsby198472-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> That same spring of 1963, however, Kennedy had sent antinuclear activist <a href="/wiki/Norman_Cousins" title="Norman Cousins">Norman Cousins</a> to Moscow to meet with Khrushchev, where he explained that the political situation in the US made it very difficult for Kennedy agree to a comprehensive ban with Khrushchev's required terms. Cousins also assured Khrushchev that though Kennedy had rejected Khrushchev's offer of three yearly inspections, he still was set on achieving a test ban.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199985_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199985-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> In March 1963, Kennedy had also held a press conference in which he re-committed to negotiations with the Soviet Union as a means of preventing rapid nuclear proliferation, which he characterized as "the greatest possible danger and hazard."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhodes200871_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodes200871-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_American_University_Commencement_Address_June_10,_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/President_Kennedy_American_University_Commencement_Address_June_10%2C_1963.jpg/220px-President_Kennedy_American_University_Commencement_Address_June_10%2C_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/President_Kennedy_American_University_Commencement_Address_June_10%2C_1963.jpg/330px-President_Kennedy_American_University_Commencement_Address_June_10%2C_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/President_Kennedy_American_University_Commencement_Address_June_10%2C_1963.jpg/440px-President_Kennedy_American_University_Commencement_Address_June_10%2C_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2632" data-file-height="2562" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_American_University_Commencement_Address_June_10,_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">Kennedy</a> at American University</div></div></div>
<p>One of Kennedy's advisors, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman_Rostow" title="Walt Whitman Rostow">Walt Whitman Rostow</a>, advised the President to make a test ban conditional on the Soviet Union withdrawing troops from <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and abiding by a 1962 agreement on <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, but Kennedy opted instead for test-ban negotiations without preconditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis1982228–229_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis1982228–229-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> On 10 June 1963, in an effort to reinvigorate and recontextualize a test ban, President Kennedy dedicated his <a href="/wiki/American_University_speech" title="American University speech">commencement address at American University</a> to "the most important topic on earth: world peace" and proceeded to make his case for the treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002900_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002900-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy first called on Americans to dispel the idea that peace is unattainable. "Let us focus instead on a more practical, more attainable peace," Kennedy said, "based not on a sudden revolution in human nature but on a gradual evolution in human institutions—on a series of concrete actions and effective agreements which are in the interest of all concerned." Second, Kennedy appealed for a new attitude towards the Soviet Union, calling Americans to not "see only a distorted and desperate view of the other side, not to see conflict as inevitable, accommodations as impossible and communication as nothing more than an exchange of threats."<sup id="cite_ref-au_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-au-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Finally, Kennedy argued for a reduction in Cold War tensions, with a test ban serving as a first step towards complete disarmament:
</p>
<blockquote><p> ...&#160;where a fresh start is badly needed—is in a treaty to outlaw nuclear tests. The conclusion of such a treaty—so near and yet so far—would check the spiraling arms race in one of its most dangerous areas. It would place the nuclear powers in a position to deal more effectively with one of the greatest hazards which man faces in 1963, the further spread of nuclear arms. It would increase our security—it would decrease the prospects of war. Surely this goal is sufficiently important to require our steady pursuit, yielding neither to the temptation to give up the whole effort nor the temptation to give up our insistence on vital and responsible safeguards.<sup id="cite_ref-au_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-au-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Kennedy proceeded to announce an agreement with Khrushchev and Macmillan to promptly resume comprehensive test-ban negotiations in Moscow and a US decision to unilaterally halt atmospheric tests.<sup id="cite_ref-au_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-au-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The speech was well received by Khrushchev, who later called it "the greatest speech by any American President since <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Roosevelt</a>,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002904_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002904-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> though was met with some skepticism within the US. The speech was endorsed by Humphrey and other Democrats, but labeled a "dreadful mistake" by Republican Senator <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> and "another case of concession" by <a href="/wiki/Everett_Dirksen" title="Everett Dirksen">Everett Dirksen</a>, the leader of the Senate Republicans. Dirksen and <a href="/wiki/Charles_A._Halleck" title="Charles A. Halleck">Charles A. Halleck</a>, the second-ranking <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House</a> Republican, warned that the renewed negotiations might end in "virtual surrender."<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to prior experience in arms control and his personal relationship with Khrushchev, former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Assistant_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Assistant Secretary of War">Assistant Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/John_J._McCloy" title="John J. McCloy">John J. McCloy</a> was first considered the likely choice for chief US negotiator in Moscow, but his name was withdrawn after he turned out to be unavailable over the summer. <a href="/wiki/W._Averell_Harriman" title="W. Averell Harriman">W. Averell Harriman</a>, a former ambassador to the Soviet Union well respected in Moscow, was chosen instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002902–903_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002902–903-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The US delegation would also include <a href="/wiki/Adrian_S._Fisher" title="Adrian S. Fisher">Adrian S. Fisher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Kaysen" title="Carl Kaysen">Carl Kaysen</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_McNaughton_(government_official)" title="John McNaughton (government official)">John McNaughton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_R._Tyler" title="William R. Tyler">William R. Tyler</a>. In Britain, Macmillan initially wanted David Ormsby-Gore, who had just completed a term as foreign minister, to lead his delegation, but there were concerns that Ormsby-Gore would appear to be a US "stooge" (Kennedy described him as "the brightest man he ever knew").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeaborg198115_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeaborg198115-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Instead, Macmillan chose <a href="/wiki/Quintin_Hogg,_Baron_Hailsham_of_St_Marylebone" title="Quintin Hogg, Baron Hailsham of St Marylebone">Quintin Hogg</a>. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.</a>, a special advisor to Kennedy, believed that Hogg was "ill prepared on the technicalities of the problem and was consumed by a desire to get a treaty at almost any cost."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002905_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002905-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Gromyko" title="Andrei Gromyko">Andrei Gromyko</a>, the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Soviet_Union)" title="Ministry of Foreign Affairs (Soviet Union)">Minister of Foreign Affairs</a>, served as Moscow's emissary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002906_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002906-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Heading into the negotiations, there was still no resolution within the Kennedy Administration of the question of whether to pursue a comprehensive or partial ban. In an effort to achieve the former, Britain proposed reducing the number of mandated inspections to allay Soviet concerns, but Harriman believed such a reduction would have to be paired with other concessions that Khrushchev would be able to show off within the Soviet Union and to China. Withdrawing <a href="/wiki/PGM-19_Jupiter" title="PGM-19 Jupiter">PGM-19 Jupiter</a> missiles from <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> would have been an option, had they not already been removed in the wake of the Cuban Missile Crisis. In meetings prior to the negotiations, Kennedy informed Harriman that he would be willing to make concessions on the <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> question.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002903–904_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002903–904-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 2 July 1963, Khrushchev proposed a partial ban on tests in the atmosphere, outer space, and underwater, which would avoid the contentious issue of detecting underground tests. Notably, Khrushchev did not link this proposal to a moratorium on underground tests (as had been proposed earlier), but said it should be followed by a <a href="/wiki/Non-aggression_pact" title="Non-aggression pact">non-aggression pact</a> between <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002904_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002904-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> "A test ban agreement combined with the signing of a non-aggression pact between the two groups of state will create a fresh international climate more favorable for a solution of the major problems of our time, including disarmament," Khrushchev said.<sup id="cite_ref-khrushchevspeech_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-khrushchevspeech-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the nuclear powers pursued a test ban agreement, they also sought to contend with a rising communist China, which at the time was pursuing its own <a href="/wiki/China_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction#Nuclear_weapons" title="China and weapons of mass destruction">nuclear program</a>. In 1955, <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> expressed to the Soviet Union his belief that China could withstand a first nuclear strike and more than 100 million casualties. In the 1950s, the Soviet Union assisted the Chinese nuclear program, but stopped short of providing China with an actual nuclear bomb, which was followed by <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">increasingly tense relations</a> in the late 1950s and early 1960s. Khrushchev began the test-ban talks of 1958 with minimal prior discussion with China, and the two countries' agreement on military-technology cooperation was terminated in June 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-wilsoncenter_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilsoncenter-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the Moscow negotiations of the summer of 1963, Kennedy granted Harriman significant latitude in reaching a "Soviet-American understanding" vis-à-vis China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002904_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002904-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Secret Sino-Soviet talks in July 1963 revealed further discord between the two communist powers, as the Soviet Union released a statement that it did not "share the views of the Chinese leadership about creating 'a thousand times higher civilization' on the corpses of hundreds of millions of people." The Soviet Union also issued an ideological critique of China's nuclear policy, declaring that China's apparent openness to nuclear war was "in crying contradiction to the idea of <a href="/wiki/Marxism-Leninism" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxism-Leninism">Marxism-Leninism</a>," as a nuclear war would "not distinguish between imperialists and working people."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002905_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002905-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The negotiations were inaugurated on 15 July 1963 at the <a href="/wiki/Kremlin" class="mw-redirect" title="Kremlin">Kremlin</a> with Khrushchev in attendance. Khrushchev reiterated that the Anglo-American inspection plan would amount to espionage, effectively dismissing the possibility of a comprehensive ban. Following the script of his 3 July 1963 speech, Khrushchev did not demand a simultaneous moratorium on underground testing and instead proposed a non-aggression pact. Under instruction from Washington, Harriman replied that the US would explore the possibility of a non-aggression pact in good faith, but indicated that while a test ban could be quickly completed, a non-aggression pact would require lengthy discussions. Additionally, such a pact would complicate the issue of Western access to <a href="/wiki/West_Berlin" title="West Berlin">West Berlin</a>. Harriman also took the opportunity to propose a non-proliferation agreement with would bar the transfer of nuclear weapons between countries. Khrushchev said that such an agreement should be considered in the future, but in the interim, a test ban would have the same effect on limiting proliferation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002905–906_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002905–906-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following initial discussions, Gromyko and Harriman began examining drafts of a test-ban agreement. First, language in the drafted preamble appeared to Harriman to prohibit the use of nuclear weapons in self-defense, which Harriman insisted be clarified. Harriman additionally demanded that an explicit clause concerning withdrawal from the agreement be added to the treaty; Khrushchev believed that each state had a sovereign right to withdraw, which should simply be assumed. Harriman informed Gromyko that without a clause governing withdrawal, which he believed the US Senate would demand, the US could not assent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002906–907_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002906–907-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, the two sides settled upon compromise language:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Each Party shall in exercising its national sovereignty have the right to withdraw from the Treaty if it decides that extraordinary events, related to the subject matter of this Treaty, have jeopardized the supreme interests of its country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002907_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002907-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Gromyko and Harriman debated how states not universally recognized (e.g., <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a> and China) could join the agreement. The US proposed asserting that accession to the treaty would not indicate international recognition. This was rejected by the Soviet Union. Eventually, with Kennedy's approval, US envoys Fisher and McNaughton devised a system whereby multiple government would serve as <a href="/wiki/Depositary" title="Depositary">depositaries</a> for the treaty, allowing individual states to sign only the agreement held by the government of their choice in association with other like-minded states. This solution, which overcame one of the more challenging roadblocks in the negotiations, also served to allay mounting concerns from Macmillan, which were relayed to Washington, that an agreement would once again be derailed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002907–908_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002907–908-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Finally, in an original Soviet draft, the signature of France would have been required for the treaty to come into effect. At Harriman's insistence, this requirement was removed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199021_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199021-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_address_on_Test_Ban_Treaty,_26_July_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/President_Kennedy_address_on_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_26_July_1963.jpg/220px-President_Kennedy_address_on_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_26_July_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/President_Kennedy_address_on_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_26_July_1963.jpg/330px-President_Kennedy_address_on_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_26_July_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/President_Kennedy_address_on_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_26_July_1963.jpg/440px-President_Kennedy_address_on_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_26_July_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="647" data-file-height="517" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_address_on_Test_Ban_Treaty,_26_July_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">Kennedy</a> announces the agreement on 26 July 1963</div></div></div>
<p>The agreement was initialed on 25 July 1963, just 10 days after negotiations commenced. The following day, Kennedy delivered a 26-minute televised address on the agreement, declaring that since the invention of nuclear weapons, "all mankind has been struggling to escape from the darkening prospect of mass destruction on earth&#160;... Yesterday a shaft of light cut into the darkness." Kennedy expressed hope that the test ban would be the first step towards broader rapprochement, limit nuclear fallout, restrict nuclear proliferation, and slow the arms race in such a way that fortifies US security. Kennedy concluded his address in reference to a Chinese <a href="/wiki/Proverb" title="Proverb">proverb</a> that he had used with Khrushchev in Vienna two years prior. "'A journey of a thousand miles must begin with a single step,'" Kennedy said. "And if that journey is a thousand miles, or even more, let history record that we, in this land, at this time, took the first step."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002910_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002910-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jfktv_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfktv-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a speech in Moscow following the agreement, Khrushchev declared that the treaty would not end the arms race and by itself could not "avert the danger of war," and reiterated his proposal of a NATO-Warsaw Pact non-aggression accord.<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> For Khrushchev, the test ban negotiations had long been a means of improving the Soviet Union's global image and reducing strain in relations with the West.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199024_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199024-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> There are also some indications that military experts within the Soviet Union saw a test ban as a way to restrict US development of <a href="/wiki/Tactical_nuclear_weapon" title="Tactical nuclear weapon">tactical nuclear weapons</a>, which could have increased US willingness to deploy small nuclear weapons on battlefields while circumventing the Soviet nuclear <a href="/wiki/Deterrence_theory" title="Deterrence theory">deterrent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199026_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199026-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Concern that a comprehensive ban would retard modernization of the Soviet arsenal may have pushed Khrushchev towards a partial ban.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199027_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199027-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Counteracting the move towards a partial ban was Khrushchev's interest in reducing spending on testing, as underground testing was more expensive than the atmospheric tests the Soviet Union had been conducting; Khrushchev preferred a comprehensive ban as it would have eliminated the cost of testing entirely.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199988_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199988-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, there was internal concern about nuclear proliferation, particularly regarding the prospect of France and China crossing the threshold and the possibility of a multilateral NATO nuclear force, which was seen as a step towards West Germany acquiring nuclear weapons (the first Soviet test ban proposal in 1955 was made in the same month than West Germany joined NATO).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199028–29_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199028–29-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was not until after the agreement was reached that the negotiators broached the question of France and China joining the treaty. Harriman proposed to Khrushchev that the US lobby France while the Soviet Union pursued a Chinese signature. "That's your problem," Khrushchev said in reply.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002908_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002908-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Earlier, the Soviet ambassador to the US, Mikhail A. Menshikov, reportedly asked whether the US could "deliver the French."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrode199029_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrode199029-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Both Kennedy and Macmillan personally called on de Gaulle to join, offering assistance to the French nuclear program in return.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966465_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966465-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, on 29 July 1963, France announced it would not join the treaty. It was followed by China two days later.<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 5 August 1963, British Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a>, Soviet foreign minister Gromyko, and US Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Dean_Rusk" title="Dean Rusk">Dean Rusk</a> signed the final agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-statedept_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="After_the_Moscow_agreement">After the Moscow agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: After the Moscow agreement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Between 8 and 27 August 1963, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Foreign_Relations" title="United States Senate Committee on Foreign Relations">United States Senate Committee on Foreign Relations</a> held hearings on the treaty. The Kennedy administration largely presented a united front in favor of the deal. Leaders of the once-opposed Joint Chiefs of Staff (JCS) and AEC acknowledged that the treaty would be of net benefit, though Teller, former members of the JCS and AEC, and the commander of the <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Air_Command" title="Strategic Air Command">Strategic Air Command</a> made clear their firm opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolsby198472_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolsby198472-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The opponents' argument centered on four themes. First, banning atmospheric tests would prevent the US from ensuring the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_hardening" title="Radiation hardening">hardness</a> of its <a href="/wiki/LGM-30_Minuteman" title="LGM-30 Minuteman">LGM-30 Minuteman</a> missile silos and, second, from developing a capable <a href="/wiki/Missile_defense" title="Missile defense">missile defense</a> system. Third, it was argued that the Soviet Union led the US in high-yield weapons (recall the Soviet Tsar Bomba test of 1961), which required atmospheric testing banned by the treaty, while the US led the Soviet Union in low-yield weapons, which were tested underground and would be permitted by the treaty. Fourth, the ban would prevent <a href="/wiki/Peaceful_nuclear_explosion" title="Peaceful nuclear explosion">peaceful, civilian uses of nuclear detonations</a>. Teller declared that the treaty would be a "step away from safety and possibly&#160;... toward war."<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_signs_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty,_07_October_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/President_Kennedy_signs_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_07_October_1963.jpg/250px-President_Kennedy_signs_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_07_October_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/President_Kennedy_signs_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_07_October_1963.jpg/375px-President_Kennedy_signs_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_07_October_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/President_Kennedy_signs_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_07_October_1963.jpg/500px-President_Kennedy_signs_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty%2C_07_October_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="694" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_signs_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty,_07_October_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">Kennedy</a> signs the PTBT on 7 October 1963 before <a href="/wiki/W._Averell_Harriman" title="W. Averell Harriman">W. Averell Harriman</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dean_Rusk" title="Dean Rusk">Dean Rusk</a>, and others</div></div></div>
<p>Administration testimony sought to counteract these arguments. Defense Secretary Robert McNamara announced his "unequivocal support" for the treaty before the Foreign Relations Committee, arguing that US nuclear forces were secure and clearly superior to those of the Soviet Union, and that any major Soviet tests would be detected. <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a>, the chairman of the AEC, also gave his support to the treaty in testimony, as did <a href="/wiki/Harold_Brown_(Secretary_of_Defense)" title="Harold Brown (Secretary of Defense)">Harold Brown</a>, the Department of Defense's lead scientist, and <a href="/wiki/Norris_Bradbury" title="Norris Bradbury">Norris Bradbury</a>, the longtime director of the Los Alamos Laboratory. <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_D._Taylor" title="Maxwell D. Taylor">Maxwell D. Taylor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff</a>, also testified in favor of the deal. Taylor and other members of the JCS, including <a href="/wiki/Curtis_LeMay" title="Curtis LeMay">Curtis LeMay</a>, had made their support for the treaty conditional on four "safeguards": (1) a continued, aggressive underground testing program, (2) continued nuclear research programs, (3) continued readiness to resume atmospheric tests, and (4) improved verification equipment. Kennedy emphasized that the US would retain the ability to use nuclear weapons in war, would not be bound by the treaty if the Soviets violated it, and would continue an aggressive underground testing program. Kennedy also stressed that a ban would be a key step in preventing nuclear war.<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The testimonies of the Joint Chiefs were seen as particularly effective in allaying concerns, as were the reassurances issued by Kennedy, who had acquired a reputation for resoluteness against the Soviet Union in the wake of the Cuban Missile Crisis. Additionally, a number of prominent <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republicans</a> came out in support of the deal, including Eisenhower, Eisenhower's vice president <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, and Senator Everett Dirksen, who had initially been skeptical of the treaty. Eisenhower's science advisor and former PSAC head, George Kistiakowsky, endorsed the treaty. Former President Harry S. Truman also lent his support. Supporters of the deal mounted a significant pressure campaign, with active lobbying in favor by a range of civilian groups, including the <a href="/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers" title="United Automobile Workers">United Automobile Workers</a>/<a href="/wiki/AFL-CIO" class="mw-redirect" title="AFL-CIO">AFL-CIO</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Action" title="Peace Action">National Committee for a Sane Nuclear Policy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_Strike_for_Peace" title="Women Strike for Peace">Women Strike for Peace</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Jewish</a> organizations. Jerome Wiesner, the chairman of PSAC, later said that this public advocacy was a primary motivation for Kennedy's push for a test ban.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199988_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199988-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Civil opposition to the deal was less prominent, though the <a href="/wiki/Veterans_of_Foreign_Wars" title="Veterans of Foreign Wars">Veterans of Foreign Wars</a> announced opposition to the deal along with the <a href="/wiki/International_Council_of_Christian_Churches" title="International Council of Christian Churches">International Council of Christian Churches</a>, which rejected a "covenant with a <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Religion in the Soviet Union">godless power</a>." Polling in late August 1963 indicated that more than 60% of Americans supported the deal while less than 20% opposed it.<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerchek1970135–136_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerchek1970135–136-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 3 September 1963, the Foreign Relations Committee approved the treaty by a 16–1 vote. On 24 September 1963, the US Senate voted 80–14 to approve ratification of the treaty, exceeding the necessary two-thirds majority by 14 votes. The Soviet Union ratified the treaty the following day with a unanimous vote of the <a href="/wiki/Presidium_of_the_Supreme_Soviet" title="Presidium of the Supreme Soviet">Presidium of the Supreme Soviet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966464_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966464-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> On 10 October 1963, the treaty entered into effect.<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-armcontrol.org_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armcontrol.org-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-unoda_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unoda-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Implementation">Implementation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Implementation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Provisions">Provisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Provisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The treaty declares as its "principal aim the speediest possible achievement of an agreement on general and complete disarmament under strict international control," and explicitly states the goal of achieving a comprehensive test ban (i.e., one which bans underground tests). The treaty permanently forbids the parties to the treaty from conducting, permitting, or encouraging any nuclear explosion in the atmosphere, outer space, or underwater, as well as "any other nuclear explosion" that threatens to send nuclear debris into another state's territory.<sup id="cite_ref-unoda_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unoda-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The wording "any other nuclear explosion" prohibited peaceful nuclear explosions, due to the difficulty in differentiating those from military tests without expanded verification measures.<sup id="cite_ref-statedept_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Per the compromise forged by US delegates Adrian S. Fisher and John McNaughton in Moscow, the third article of the treaty allows states to deposit instruments of ratification or accession with the government of Britain, the Soviet Union, or US, thereby avoiding the issue of the treaty appearing to legitimize governments lacking universal recognition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002907–908_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002907–908-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> The fourth article reflects the compromise struck by Gromyko and Harriman in Moscow on departure from the treaty. It recognizes the sovereign right of states to withdraw from treaties, as Khrushchev argued, while explicitly granting parties the right to withdraw if "extraordinary events&#160;... have jeopardized the supreme interests of its country," per the US demand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002906–907_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger2002906–907-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-unoda_169-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unoda-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Signatories">Signatories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Signatories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_parties_to_the_Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="List of parties to the Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty">List of parties to the Partial Nuclear Test Ban Treaty</a></div>
<p>By 15 April 1964, 6 months after the PTBT went into effect, more than 100 states had joined the treaty as signatories and 39 had ratified or acceded to it.<sup id="cite_ref-cq_123-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The most recent party to the PTBT is <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>, which succeeded to the treaty in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-unoda_169-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unoda-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 126 states were party to the treaty, with 10 other states having signed but not deposited instruments of ratification. Sixty states have not signed the PTBT, including the nuclear states of China, France, and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-siglist_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-siglist-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, an ideological ally of China at the time of the PTBT's enactment, also has not signed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966464_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsonStein1966464-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-siglist_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-siglist-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effectiveness">Effectiveness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Effectiveness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Radiocarbon_bomb_spike.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Radiocarbon_bomb_spike.svg/300px-Radiocarbon_bomb_spike.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Radiocarbon_bomb_spike.svg/450px-Radiocarbon_bomb_spike.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Radiocarbon_bomb_spike.svg/600px-Radiocarbon_bomb_spike.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Radiocarbon_bomb_spike.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">carbon-14</a> (<sup>14</sup>C) in the Southern and Northern Hemispheres. Atmospheric testing almost doubled the concentration of <sup>14</sup>C in the Northern Hemisphere.<sup id="cite_ref-radiocarbon_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radiocarbon-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The PTBT's ratification coincided with the beginning of a steep decline in the presence of radioactive particles in the atmosphere (following a "bomb spike" in the early 1960s), but did not halt nuclear proliferation.<sup id="cite_ref-atomicheritage_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atomicheritage-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-radiocarbon_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radiocarbon-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013532_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnsSiracusa2013532-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> One year after the PTBT's entry into force, China (a non-signatory) conducted the <a href="/wiki/596_(nuclear_test)" class="mw-redirect" title="596 (nuclear test)">596 test</a> and became the world's fifth nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Since China, <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons" title="List of states with nuclear weapons">four other states</a> are known or believed to have acquired nuclear weapons. However, the PTBT has been credited with <i>slowing</i> proliferation due to the greater expense associated with underground tests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhodes200872_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodes200872-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy had warned in 1963 that without a test ban there could be 10 nuclear states by 1970 and 15–20 by 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The decade following ratification of the PTBT (1963–72) featured more US nuclear tests than the decade prior (1953–62). In the following decade, the US conducted 385 nuclear tests and 23 peaceful nuclear explosions (PNEs), versus 268 tests and three PNEs in the preceding decade. In contrast, the number of Soviet detonations fell from 218 in the preceding decade to 157 in the following decade, as the Soviet Union was never able to meet the pace of US underground explosions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199985–86_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista199985–86-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> China and France, both non-signatories, conducted 53 tests between 1963 and 1973. In all, 436 tests were conducted between the signing of the PTBT and 1 July 1973, compared to 499 tests between 16 July 1945 and the signing of the PTBT.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> In the 1960s and 1970s, China conducted 22 atmospheric tests and France conducted 50.<sup id="cite_ref-nsarchive_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsarchive-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The last atmospheric test was conducted by China in 1980, after French atmospheric testing stopped in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nsa2_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsa2-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Public opposition to nuclear testing continued following the treaty's enactment. <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> was founded in 1971 in opposition to a planned underground test on the <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaskan</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Amchitka" title="Amchitka">Amchitka</a>. In 1982, a Greenpeace ship docked at Leningrad without permission demanding that the Soviet Union stop testing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvangelista1999168–170_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvangelista1999168–170-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The PTBT was a first of a series of nuclear arms control treaties in the second half of 20th century.
