The PTBT has been considered the stepping stone to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Non-Proliferation_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons">Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons</a> (NPT) of 1968, which made explicit reference to the progress provided by the PTBT.<sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In addition to the NPT, the PTBT was followed within ten years by the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty" title="Outer Space Treaty">Outer Space Treaty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tlatelolco" title="Treaty of Tlatelolco">Treaty of Tlatelolco</a> in 1967, <a href="/wiki/Seabed_Arms_Control_Treaty" title="Seabed Arms Control Treaty">Seabed Arms Control Treaty</a> in 1971, and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Ballistic_Missile_Treaty" title="Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty">Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty</a> in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In 1974, the <a href="/wiki/Threshold_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="Threshold Test Ban Treaty">Threshold Test Ban Treaty</a> prohibited underground tests with yields above 150 kilotons.<sup id="cite_ref-nsa2_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsa2-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 1977, the original parties to the PTBT renewed discussion of a comprehensive test ban in Geneva. Through the end of the 1970s, the US, UK, and Soviet Union reached agreement on draft provisions prohibiting all testing, temporarily banning PNEs, and establishing a verification system including on-site inspections. However, the sides remained at odds over the precise details of verification and talks would permanently disband with the departure of President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Momentum towards a comprehensive ban reemerged under <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a> and President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, with Gorbachev initiating a testing moratorium in 1985. In December 1986, the US indicated support for the "long-term objective" of a comprehensive ban, followed by the commencement of testing negotiations between the US and Soviet Union in November 1987. In December 1987, the US and Soviet Union agreed to a joint program of experiments on detecting underground tests.<sup id="cite_ref-faschron_64-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faschron-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> In August 1988, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a> petitioned to transform the PTBT into a comprehensive ban by extending the treaty to underground tests. At a conference on the plan in January 1991, the US indicated it would not permit efforts to achieve a comprehensive ban by consensus with amendments to the PTBT.<sup id="cite_ref-statedept_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout the 1990s, progress accelerated towards a comprehensive test ban treaty (CTBT). Following a series of international meetings on the subject, the <a href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="UN General Assembly">UN General Assembly</a> approved resolution 50/64, which appealed for states to follow the PTBT and called for conclusion of the CTBT talks. In September 1996, the <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Nuclear-Test-Ban_Treaty" title="Comprehensive Nuclear-Test-Ban Treaty">Comprehensive Nuclear-Test-Ban Treaty</a> was signed and superseded the PTBT, though the PTBT is still in effect for states not party to the CTBT.<sup id="cite_ref-nti_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nti-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The CTBT has yet to enter into force, as 8 required states have not ratified the treaty, including the US and China. France, Russia, and the UK have ratified the CTBT.<sup id="cite_ref-nti2_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nti2-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The technology for detecting underground tests has significantly improved since the 1950s and 1960s, with monitors detecting tests down to 1 kiloton with a high degree of confidence.<sup id="cite_ref-nsarchive_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsarchive-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Violations_and_accidents">Violations and accidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Violations and accidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Early compliance with the PTBT was believed to be good,<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> though there have been a number of accidental releases of nuclear debris into the atmosphere by parties to the treaty. Additionally, "venting" of underground tests by the US and Soviet Union also continued to release radioactive debris into the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-nsa2_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsa2-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Fully contained underground tests were not wholly "clean" either. Underground testing reduced the risk caused by <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclides</a> with short <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Iodine-131" title="Iodine-131">iodine-131</a>, and is generally safer than other forms of testing. However, underground testing may also cause long-lived radionuclides, including <a href="/wiki/Caesium-135" class="mw-redirect" title="Caesium-135">caesium-135</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iodine-129" title="Iodine-129">iodine-129</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, to seep into the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A notable atmospheric release of radioactive gas followed the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Chagan_(nuclear_test)" title="Chagan (nuclear test)">Chagan test</a> of 15 January 1965 in present-day <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>. Roughly 20% of the radioactive debris produced by the 140-kiloton detonation was released into the atmosphere, with some fallout occurring over Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The US complained to Moscow, but no subsequent action was taken.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> On 25 April 1966, the <i>Pin Stripe</i> underground test in Nevada (part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Flintlock_(nuclear_test)" title="Operation Flintlock (nuclear test)">Operation Flintlock</a>) experienced a venting malfunction and produced a radioactive plume headed towards the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwestern US</a>; the AEC determined that the test did not threaten human health.<sup id="cite_ref-nsa2_179-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsa2-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another accidental release occurred following the <a href="/wiki/Yucca_Flat#Baneberry" title="Yucca Flat"><i>Baneberry</i></a> shot at the Nevada Test Site on 18 December 1970 (part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Emery" title="Operation Emery">Operation Emery</a>). The 10-kiloton underground detonation produced a fissure in the ground, which subsequently allowed radioactive gas to escape into the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-bane2_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bane2-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Radioactive material released by the fissure reached an altitude of 10,000 feet (3,000&#160;m) and exposed 86 workers to radiation, though none at excessive levels.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The incident has since been described as one of the "world's worst nuclear disasters."<sup id="cite_ref-bane_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bane-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Declassified US documents indicate that the US may have violated the PTBT's ban on atmospheric testing in 1972 by, at the instruction of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a>, monitoring and collecting data on French atmospheric tests over the Pacific Ocean, which may have amounted to cooperation with the French program. Declassified documents also indicate that the US and UK circumvented the prescribed verification system in 1964–65 by establishing a series of additional control posts in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nsa2_179-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsa2-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1979 <a href="/wiki/Vela_Incident" class="mw-redirect" title="Vela Incident">Vela Incident</a> in the southern Atlantic Ocean may have been an atmospheric nuclear test in contravention of the PTBT by <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and South Africa, both of which were parties to the treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-velanyt_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-velanyt-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-velabulletin_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-velabulletin-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Technical_Applications_Center" title="Air Force Technical Applications Center">Air Force Technical Applications Center</a> – US organization dedicated to monitoring nuclear treaty compliance</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Boeing_WC-135_Constant_Phoenix" title="Boeing WC-135 Constant Phoenix">Boeing WC-135 Constant Phoenix</a> – US aircraft used to detect nuclear explosions</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partial_Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
